Point of View 
Maurice de Gosson 
Faculty of Mathematics, NuHAG group, Senior Researcher at the University 
of Vienna 
https://homepage.univie.ac.at/maurice.de.gosson 
Abstract: Gaussian states play a ubiquitous role in quantum information theory 
and in quantum optics because they are easy to manufacture in the laboratory, 
and have in addition important extremality properties. Of particular interest are 
their separability properties. Even if major advances have been made in their 
study in recent years, the topic is still largely open. In this talk we will discuss 
separability questions for Gaussian states from a rigorous point of view using 
symplectic geometry, and present some new results and properties.

and Convex Optimisation in Hadamard 
Manifolds 
Salem Said1(B), Nicolas Le Bihan2, and Jonathan H. Manton3 
1 CNRS, Laboratoire IMS, Talence, France 
salem.said@u-bordeaux.fr 
2 CNRS, GIPSA-lab, Talence, France 
nicolas.le-bihan@gipsa-lab.grenoble-inp.fr 
3 The University of Melbourne, Melbourne, Australia 
jmanton@unimelb.edu.au 
Abstract. The present work is motivated by the problem of Bayesian 
inference for Gaussian distributions in symmetric Hadamard spaces (that 
is, Hadamard manifolds which are also symmetric spaces). To investigate 
this problem, it introduces new tools for Markov Chain Monte Carlo, and 
convex optimisation: (1) it provides easy-to-verify suf i cient conditions 
for the geometric ergodicity of an isotropic Metropolis-Hastings Markov 
chain, in a symmetric Hadamard space, (2) it shows how the Riemannian 
gradient descent method can achieve an exponential rate of convergence, 
when applied to a strongly convex function, on a Hadamard manifold. 
Using these tools, two Bayesian estimators, maximum-a-posteriori and 
minimum-mean-squares, are compared. When the underlying Hadamard 
manifold is a space of constant negative curvature, they are found to be 
surprisingly close to each other. This leads to an open problem: are these 
two estimators, in fact, equal (assuming constant negative curvature)? 
Keywords: Hadamard manifoldÂ·Gaussian distributionÂ·Bayesian 
inferenceÂ·MCMCÂ·Convex 
optimisation

From Bayesian Inference to MCMC and Convex Optimisation in Hadamard ManifoldsAuthors:Salem Said, CNRS, Laboratoire IMS, Talence, FranceNicolas Le Bihan, CNRS, GIPSA-lab, Talence, FranceJonathan H. Manton, The University of Melbourne, Melbourne, AustraliaAbstract:
The study is driven by the problem of Bayesian inference for Gaussian distributions within symmetric Hadamard spaces. This requires the introduction of new tools for Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) and convex optimization. Two primary contributions are:Introducing sufficient conditions for the geometric ergodicity of an isotropic Metropolis-Hastings Markov chain within a symmetric Hadamard space.Demonstrating that the Riemannian gradient descent method can achieve exponential rates of convergence when applied to a strongly convex function on a Hadamard manifold.The paper then compares two Bayesian estimators: the maximum-a-posteriori (MAP) and the minimum-mean-squares (MMS). In cases where the underlying Hadamard manifold has constant negative curvature, these estimators are very close in value. The paper raises the open question of whether the two estimators are, in fact, the same when assuming constant negative curvature.Keywords:
Hadamard manifold, Gaussian distribution, Bayesian inference, MCMC, Convex optimizationMain Content:
The paper further delves into the specifics of Gaussian distributions on symmetric Hadamard spaces. A Gaussian distribution on this space is defined with a given probability density function related to the Riemannian volume. The maximum-likelihood estimator for a parameter, based on independent samples, is found to be the Riemannian barycentre of the samples. The paper then contrasts the maximum-likelihood estimator with a Bayesian approach for estimating a parameter. This Bayesian approach uses a prior density that is Gaussian.It's worth noting that the paper seems to be making significant contributions to the area of Bayesian inference within the context of Hadamard manifolds. The use of MCMC techniques and convex optimization tools to study this problem reflects the mathematical sophistication of the subject matter.If you have any specific questions or areas you would like further information on, please let me know!1 / 13Metropolis-Hastings

Here's a concise overview:Metropolis-Hastings Algorithm:Purpose: The Metropolis-Hastings algorithm is a method for obtaining samples from a distribution, in this case, the posterior density Ï(x)\pi(x)Ï(x). The true form of Ï(x)\pi(x)Ï(x) may not be known completely, but the MH algorithm can sample from it by using a proposal distribution and an acceptance rule.Transition Kernel: The MH algorithm uses a transition kernel, denoted as PPP, to decide how the next sample in the Markov chain should be generated. The kernel is influenced by the acceptance probability Î±(x,y)\alpha(x,y)Î±(x,y) and the proposal density q(x,y)q(x,y)q(x,y).Isotropy: In the discussed context, the proposal distribution is isotropic, meaning it only depends on the distance between x and y, not their absolute positions.Goal: One of the main objectives of the MH algorithm is to ensure that the generated Markov chain is geometrically ergodic. This means that the chain converges to the desired distribution Ï\piÏ at a geometric (or exponential) rate.Properties of Geometrically Ergodic Chains:The distribution of the chain converges to Ï\piÏ at a geometric rate.The chain satisfies the strong law of large numbers, which means the average of the function values from the chain will converge to the expected value of the function under the distribution Ï\piÏ.A corresponding central limit theorem is also satisfied, which informs about the distribution of the average of the function values.Conditions for Geometric Ergodicity: The paper references another work ([7], Chapter 4) that proves a proposition about the conditions required for the isotropic Metropolis-Hastings chain to be geometrically ergodic. Three assumptions (a1, a2, and a3) are stated, involving specific properties of the symmetric Hadamard space and the involved functions. If these conditions hold, then the MH chain will be geometrically ergodic.Posterior Density Observations: A remark is made that the posterior density Ï\piÏ in the equation (3) meets the conditions (a1) and (a2) presented earlier. Some mathematical derivations related to gradient functions are also provided to support this claim.Impression:
The discussion provides a rigorous look into the application of the Metropolis-Hastings algorithm in a specific mathematical setting, namely symmetric Hadamard spaces. The conditions for the geometric ergodicity of the MH chain are vital as they ensure that the chain will converge to the desired distribution at a guaranteed rate. This is critical in Bayesian inference, where accurate sampling from the posterior distribution is crucial for making informed decisions.Let me know if you'd like more details on any specific part or any other topic!1 / 14The Empirical Barycentre

Def i nition 
A Hadamard manifold is a Riemannian manifold which is complete, simply 
connected, and has negative sectional curvatures [5]. Completeness means that 
geodesics extend indef i nitely in both directions, and is equivalent to the prop- 
erty that closed and bounded sets are compact (by the Hopf-Rinow theorem [5]). 
The simple connectedness and negative curvature properties combine to ensure 
that the Riemannian exponential map is a dif f eomorphism. In particular, any 
two points x,y in a Hadamard manifold M are connected by a unique length- 
minimising geodesic c : [0,1] â M, with c(0) = x, and dc/dt|t=0= 
Expâ1 
x (y). 
Convexity

t â [0,1], the point x#ty 
= c(t) is said to be a geodesic convex combination of 
x and y, with respective weights (1ât) and t. In a Hadamard manifold [5], any 
open geodesic ball B(xâ,R) (or any closed geodesic ball Â¯ 
B(xâ,R)) is convex: if 
x and y belong to this ball, so does x#ty for all t â (0,1). 
A function f : M â R is called (Î±/2)-strongly convex, if (f â¦ c)(t) is an 
(Î±/2)-strongly convex function of t, for any geodesic c : [0,1] â M. That is, if

A.2Symmetric Spaces 
Def i nition 
A Riemannian symmetric space is a Riemannian manifold M, such that for each 
x â M there exists an isometry sx: M â M, which i xes x and reverses geodesics 
passing through x: if c is a geodesic curve with c(0) = x, then sx(c(t)) = c(ât). 
As a consequence of this def i nition, a Riemannian symmetric space is always 
complete and homogeneous. Completeness was discussed in AppendixA.1. On 
the other hand, homogeneity means that for each x and y in M, there exists 
an isometry g : M â M, with g(x) = y. A symmetric space M may always be 
expressed as a quotient manifold M = G/K, where G is a certain Lie group of 
isometries of M, and K is the subgroup of elements of G which i x some point 
x â M.

The structure of these manifolds is central to understanding the geometry and topology of the spaces they define.Geodesics:In the context of Hadamard manifolds, geodesics play a significant role. They represent the shortest paths between points. In symplectic geometry, geodesics also play a role, especially when understanding Hamiltonian dynamics where the flow along a Hamiltonian vector field can be thought of in terms of geodesics.Curvature:Hadamard manifolds have negative sectional curvatures. In the context of symplectic geometry, curvature, especially the curvature of certain canonical bundles, can play a role in understanding the global properties of the manifold. For instance, KÃ¤hler manifolds, which are both symplectic and complex, have a curvature form that is closed.Convexity:Convexity in Hadamard manifolds, as you've mentioned, is related to geodesics and the property that geodesic balls are convex. In symplectic geometry, convexity properties can arise when studying moment maps and symplectic reductions, which are fundamental in understanding the geometry and topology of symplectic manifolds.Applications in Physics:Symplectic geometry has deep ties to classical mechanics through the study of Hamiltonian systems. The symplectic structure captures the essence of the conservation of energy and momentum. While Hadamard manifolds and symmetric spaces may not be as directly tied to physical systems, they do provide insights into the geometric properties of spaces that can be applied in various contexts.Group Actions:Symmetric spaces involve the action of a group (the isometry group) on a manifold. Similarly, in symplectic geometry, group actions, especially those of Lie groups, are prevalent. For example, when studying symplectic reductions or understanding the geometry of moduli spaces.Isometries and Diffeomorphisms:Both Hadamard manifolds and symmetric spaces emphasize the importance of isometries. In symplectic geometry, diffeomorphisms preserving the symplectic structure (symplectic diffeomorphisms) play a crucial role, much like isometries in Riemannian geometry.To delve deeper into the connections and see them in action, one would typically need to study specific problems or examples where these concepts intertwine. However, at a high level, both Hadamard manifolds and symplectic geometry offer perspectives on the rich tapestry of structures and properties that differentiable manifolds can exhibit.1 / 1IGromovâs Nonsqueezing Theorem1

Let's unpack some of these:Gromovâs Nonsqueezing Theorem: This theorem, often simply known as Gromov's Nonsqueezing, is a fundamental result in symplectic geometry. It states that a ball in a symplectic vector space cannot be symplectically embedded into a cylinder of the same height but with a smaller radius. This theorem reveals a deep geometric constraint that symplectic structures impose, which is in contrast to the flexibility we see in general topology.Liouvilleâs theorem in Hamiltonian mechanics: Liouville's theorem is a foundational result in classical mechanics which states that the volume in phase space is preserved under the flow of a Hamiltonian system.Symplectomorphisms and symplectic embeddings: These are diffeomorphisms that preserve the symplectic structure. They play a crucial role in the study of the geometry and dynamics of symplectic manifolds.Almost complex structures: An almost complex structure on a manifold is a bundle endomorphism that squares to -1. When paired with a symplectic structure, it can be used to define holomorphic curves, which are central to Gromov's methods.Holomorphic curves: These are mappings from Riemann surfaces into almost complex manifolds that are holomorphic with respect to the given almost complex structure. They have become a central tool in symplectic topology due to their many applications, including the proof of Gromov's nonsqueezing theorem.Compactness: This appears to focus on the compactness properties of spaces of holomorphic curves, which is crucial for understanding their moduli spaces and the associated Gromov-Witten invariants.Symplectic energy, action, and other functionals: These are essential tools in symplectic geometry and topology to study dynamical properties, invariants, and constraints on symplectic and Hamiltonian systems.Moduli space of J-holomorphic discs: This refers to the space of all J-holomorphic discs modulo certain equivalence relations. Moduli spaces play a crucial role in many areas of mathematics and have deep connections with algebraic geometry, string theory, and representation theory.The topics covered provide a good depth of insight into the powerful interplay between analysis, geometry, and topology in the symplectic setting. Gromov's methods, which revolve around the use of pseudoholomorphic curves, have revolutionized the field of symplectic geometry, leading to a plethora of new discoveries and invariants.1 / 1Elliptic regularity . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .113

The holomorphic discs in the title of this book refer to a mathematical object 
that is, strictly speaking, neither holomorphic nor a disc. This is in accordance 
with the principle, as laid down by [Hirsch 1976], that âin mathematics a red 
herring does not have to be either red or a herringâ. 
By discs we mean smooth maps def i ned on the unit disc in the complex 
plane C, taking values in some manifold. We shall be speaking of curves as smooth 
maps def i ned on a domain (or its closure) in the complex plane1. More generally, 
the domain of def i nition may be a Riemann surface (possibly with boundary); such 
curves will not be considered in the present text, though. 
The usage of the word holomorphic requires a more detailed explication. 
In the strict sense of the word, this refers to curves where the target space is a 
complex manifold, and the map satisf i es the CauchyâRiemann equations. Equiva- 
lently, these are maps whose dif f erential at any point of the domain of def i nition 
is a complex linear map. In the main body of the text, we shall adhere to this 
narrow interpretation of the word âholomorphicâ. 
The holomorphic discs of the title in point of fact refer to the more general 
pseudoholomorphic or J-holomorphic maps. The idea behind this concept is quite 
simple. In order to speak sensibly of maps with complex linear dif f erential, there is 
no need for (local) complex coordinates on the target space. All that is required is 
a notion of complex multiplication on each tangent space, which is precisely what 
an almost complex structure J on a manifold amounts to. (In two real dimensions, 
any almost complex structure comes from complex coordinates, thanks to the 
existence of isothermal coordinates2for any conformal structure; for this reason 
we need not consider a wider class of domains of def i nition.) 
Beware that many authors nowadays use âholomorphic curveâ with the latter, 
wider meaning. To keep the title of this book snappy, we have done the same there, 
but there only. 
The ground-breaking observation in [Gromov 1985] was that pseudoholomor- 
phic curves share many desirable features with holomorphic curves, provided that 
the almost complex structure J is compatible, in a sense to be def i ned, with a sym- 
plectic form Ï (that is, a closed nondegenerate dif f erential 2-form). This includes

them being minimal surfaces, and energy estimates that guarantee the compact- 
ness of certain âmoduli spacesâ of pseudoholomorphic curves. Such compactness 
results pave the way for cobordism- or degree-theoretic topological arguments. 
Gromovâs i rst application of the theory of pseudoholomorphic curves in sym- 
plectic manifolds was the nonsqueezing theorem, which may be regarded as the big 
bang for the new i eld of symplectic topology. 
Symplectic geometry is a time-honoured subject, as it provides the mathe- 
matical framework for classical mechanics; see [Arnolâd 1989]. Liouvilleâs theorem 
on the preservation of volume in phase space is in fact a statement about symplec- 
tic mappings. The nonsqueezing theorem demonstrates that symplectic mappings 
are considerably more restrained than volume-preserving ones. It says that one cannot squeeze a ball (in the standard symplectic space R2n) into a cylinder of 
smaller radius by a symplectic embedding.1 / 1ChatGPTThe preface of this book introduces the reader to the concept of "holomorphic discs" in the context of symplectic geometry and topology. The book you hold in your hands (if you are an old-fashioned reader) presents 
a complete proof of the nonsqueezing theorem. In Chapter I we expand on the 
classical context just mentioned and introduce all the concepts required to give a 
precise formulation of the theorem and a sketch of its proof. 
The later chapters successively i ll in the details of this proof. Given a sym- 
plectic embedding of a ball of radius r into a cylinder of radius R, the aim is to 
show that r â¤ R. Ultimately, the proof boils down to establishing the existence 
of a J-holomorphic disc passing through the centre of the embedded ball, and 
whose boundary is mapped to the boundary of the cylinder. The almost complex 
structure J in question is adapted to the embedding of the ball. Energy estimates 
and an isoperimetric inequality for holomorphic discs then combine to yield the 
desired inequality on the radii. 
The existence of such a disc rests crucially on the properties of the mod- 
uli space of all pseudoholomorphic discs with boundary behaviour as described. 
Broadly speaking, chapters II and III are devoted to establishing the compactness 
of this space; chapter V deals with the manifold property of this space. A major 
complication in the proof stems from the fact that one cannot work solely within 
the framework of smooth discs, as spaces of smooth maps fail to be Banach mani- 
folds. Chapter IV shows how to resolve this issue by considering spaces of weakly 
dif f erentiable pseudoholomorphic curves and then establishing regularity results 
for such curves. 
The nonsqueezing theorem is very easy to state, and the formidable mathe- 
matical ef f ort we need to expend on its proof may seem surprising and daunting. 
In order to assuage the intellectual strain, at several places in the text we pause 
to explain why one cannot do without the intricate, mostly analytic tools that we 
introduce.1 / 1ChatGPTOutline of the TextThe book provides a comprehensive proof of the nonsqueezing theorem. Pseudoholomorphic discs made their i rst appearance in applications to con- 
tact topology, where one works with the symplectisation of a contact manifold. 
This symplectisation carries an exact symplectic form and therefore does not ad-

[Abbas & Hofer 2019].

The theory of pseudoholomorphic curves draws on methods and results, some of 
them quite deep, from diverse areas of mathematics. We have tried to develop 
the theory from i rst principles wherever feasible, but it is impossible to include 
everything without the book becoming encyclopaedic. 
The i rst two chapters can probably be understood with little more than a 
background in complex analysis. In fact, we have read this part prof i tably (for 
all involved) with students in the third and i nal year of their Bachelor stud- 
ies. Advanced calculus, which German students take in their second and third 
semesters, is taken for granted. This includes concepts such as (sub-)manifolds, 
tangent spaces, dif f erential forms, and Stokesâs theorem (especially in the diver- 
gence version known as the theorem of GauÃ). 
Everything we require from point-set topology can be found in [JÂ¨ anich 1996]: 
compactness vs. sequential compactness, continuity vs. sequential continuity, i rst 
and second countability axioms for topological spaces. 
Concerning analysis on manifolds, we freely (but sparingly) use concepts such 
as the l ow of a vector i eld and the Lie derivative (including Cartanâs formula). 
Where possible, we provide alternative and more elementary arguments. 
The remaining chapters require some basic concepts and results from linear 
functional analysis, such as Banach spaces, bounded (a.k.a. continuous) linear 
operators, and the open mapping theorem. In a few places, and in passing, we quote 
the HahnâBanach theorem. Everything we use can be found in the undergraduate 
text [BollobÂ´ as 1999] or the slightly more advanced [Hirzebruch & Scharlau 1971]. 
We need much less than these books cover. For instance, all the prerequisites from 
Fredholm theory are introduced in Chapter V, even though they can be found in 
the text by Hirzebruch and Scharlau. 
As concerns Sobolev spaces, some acquaintance with the fundamental con- 
cepts in [Adams & Fournier 2003] is certainly a boon, but not required. We quote 
without proof some Sobolev inequalities and embedding theorems, but the theory 
of weakly dif f erentiable functions is developed from scratch in Chapter IV. 
From dif f erential geometry, we use the concept of geodesics without expla- 
nation, but we do discuss the exponential map â the only reason we need to 
talk about geodesics â in detail. We also use some concepts from elementary 
dif f erential geometry such as the curvature of plane curves. 
Concerning dif f erential topological methods, we occasionally apply them with- 
out being overly loquacious. Books such as [BrÂ¨ ocker & JÂ¨ anich 1973], [Hirsch 1976], 
or [Wall 2016] cover much more than what is needed here, but a certain acquain- 
tance with one of these books certainly helps to smooth some corners in the text.

The mapping degree theory in Chapter V is developed in detail, following a dif- 
ferential topological route. The alternative approach, using homological methods, 
is sketched for readers familiar with the relevant algebraic topology.

The mathematical prerequisites ensure that readers are equipped to navigate the complex landscape of these topics, drawing connections between foundational knowledge and advanced mathematical tools.1 / 1Although they can be defined for any almost complex manifold, pseudoholomorphic curves are especially interesting when {\displaystyle J}J interacts with a symplectic form {\displaystyle \omega }\omega . An almost complex structure {\displaystyle J}J is said to be {\displaystyle \omega }\omega -tame if and only if

{\displaystyle \omega (v,Jv)>0}{\displaystyle \omega (v,Jv)>0}
for all nonzero tangent vectors {\displaystyle v}v. Tameness implies that the formula

{\displaystyle (v,w)={\frac {1}{2}}\left(\omega (v,Jw)+\omega (w,Jv)\right)}{\displaystyle (v,w)={\frac {1}{2}}\left(\omega (v,Jw)+\omega (w,Jv)\right)}
defines a Riemannian metric on {\displaystyle X}X. Gromov showed that, for a given {\displaystyle \omega }\omega , the space of {\displaystyle \omega }\omega -tame {\displaystyle J}J is nonempty and contractible. He used this theory to prove a non-squeezing theorem concerning symplectic embeddings of spheres into cylinders.

Gromov showed that certain moduli spaces of pseudoholomorphic curves (satisfying additional specified conditions) are compact, and described the way in which pseudoholomorphic curves can degenerate when only finite energy is assumed. (The finite energy condition holds most notably for curves with a fixed homology class in a symplectic manifold where J is {\displaystyle \omega }\omega -tame or {\displaystyle \omega }\omega -compatible). This Gromov compactness theorem, now greatly generalized using stable maps, makes possible the definition of GromovâWitten invariants, which count pseudoholomorphic curves in symplectic manifolds.

Compact moduli spaces of pseudoholomorphic curves are also used to construct Floer homology, which Andreas Floer (and later authors, in greater generality) used to prove the famous conjecture of Vladimir Arnol'd concerning the number of fixed points of Hamiltonian flows.1 / 1ChatGPTAbsolutely! From foundational theorems to advanced applications, these curves offer a rich mathematical tapestry that intersects with various domains in mathematics and mathematical physics.1 / 1In type II string theory, one considers surfaces traced out by strings as they travel along paths in a CalabiâYau 3-fold. Following the path integral formulation of quantum mechanics, one wishes to compute certain integrals over the space of all such surfaces. Because such a space is infinite-dimensional, these path integrals are not mathematically well-defined in general. However, under the A-twist one can deduce that the surfaces are parametrized by pseudoholomorphic curves, and so the path integrals reduce to integrals over moduli spaces of pseudoholomorphic curves (or rather stable maps), which are finite-dimensional. In closed type IIA string theory, for example, these integrals are precisely the GromovâWitten invariants.1 / 1ChatGPTAbsolutely! This ties back the vast realm of string theory with the intricate mathematical structures of symplectic geometry and pseudoholomorphic curves. Hereâs a breakdown to connect the topics:Type II String Theory and CalabiâYau 3-fold:Type II string theory refers to two supersymmetric string theories in ten dimensions. The surfaces traced out by strings, called world-sheets, can be embedded in compact manifolds known as CalabiâYau 3-folds, which have particularly special geometric properties suitable for compactifying the extra dimensions in string theory.Path Integral Formulation:The path integral approach is fundamental in quantum mechanics, especially in quantum field theory. It seeks to compute the probability amplitude by summing (integrating) over all possible histories of a system. In string theory, this means integrating over all possible world-sheets.A-Twist and Pseudoholomorphic Curves:The A-twist (or A-model) is a particular topological twist in string theory. When applied to a string world-sheet theory, it constrains the maps (from the world-sheet to the target space, i.e., CalabiâYau manifold) to be pseudoholomorphic. This is the deep connection between string theory and the previously discussed topic of pseudoholomorphic curves. This constraint greatly simplifies the infinite-dimensional problem of integrating over all world-sheets.Integrals Over Moduli Spaces and GromovâWitten Invariants:The moduli space of pseudoholomorphic curves (or stable maps, to include certain degenerate situations) is a finite-dimensional space that parametrizes these curves. Thus, our previously ill-defined infinite-dimensional path integrals become well-defined integrals over these moduli spaces.In the context of closed type IIA string theory, these integrals compute the GromovâWitten invariants. As mentioned earlier, these invariants count pseudoholomorphic curves in symplectic manifolds, and here, they have a direct physical interpretation in terms of string amplitudes.The intertwining of advanced concepts from string theory with the rich mathematical structures of symplectic geometry is a prime example of the deep connections between modern physics and mathematics. Concepts like pseudoholomorphic curves, initially developed purely for mathematical reasons, find unexpected and profound applications in our quest to understand the fundamental nature of the universe.1 / 1The role of the characteristic function in the theory of meromorphic functions in the plane is similar to that of

{\displaystyle \log M(r,f)=\log \max _{|z|\leq r}|f(z)|\,}\log M(r,f)=\log \max _{{|z|\leq r}}|f(z)|\,
in the theory of entire functions. In fact, it is possible to directly compare T(r,f) and M(r,f) for an entire function:

{\displaystyle T(r,f)\leq \log ^{+}M(r,f)\,}T(r,f)\leq \log ^{+}M(r,f)\,
and

{\displaystyle \log M(r,f)\leq \left({\dfrac {R+r}{R-r}}\right)T(R,f),\,}\log M(r,f)\leq \left({\dfrac  {R+r}{R-r}}\right)T(R,f),\,
for any R > r.

If f is a rational function of degree d, then T(r,f) ~ d log r; in fact, T(r,f) = O(log r) if and only if f is a rational function.

The order of a meromorphic function is defined by

{\displaystyle \rho (f)=\limsup _{r\rightarrow \infty }{\dfrac {\log ^{+}T(r,f)}{\log r}}.}\rho (f)=\limsup _{{r\rightarrow \infty }}{\dfrac  {\log ^{+}T(r,f)}{\log r}}.
Functions of finite order constitute an important subclass which was much studied.

When the radius R of the disc |z| â¤ R, in which the meromorphic function is defined, is finite, the Nevanlinna characteristic may be bounded. Functions in a disc with bounded characteristic, also known as functions of bounded type, are exactly those functions that are ratios of bounded analytic functions. Functions of bounded type may also be so defined for another domain such as the upper half-plane.1 / 1ChatGPTThe discussion revolves around the theory of meromorphic functions and their characteristic properties, especially when compared to entire functions. Understanding the growth and behavior of these functions is crucial for various applications in both pure and applied mathematics.1 / 1In mathematics, in particular the subfield of algebraic geometry, a rational map or rational mapping is a kind of partial function between algebraic varieties. This article uses the convention that varieties are irreducible.


Definition
Edit
Formal definition
Edit
Formally, a rational map {\displaystyle f\colon V\to W}f\colon V\to W between two varieties is an equivalence class of pairs {\displaystyle (f_{U},U)}(f_{U},U) in which {\displaystyle f_{U}}f_{U} is a morphism of varieties from a non-empty open set {\displaystyle U\subset V}U\subset V to {\displaystyle W}W, and two such pairs {\displaystyle (f_{U},U)}(f_{U},U) and {\displaystyle ({f'}_{U'},U')}{\displaystyle ({f'}_{U'},U')} are considered equivalent if {\displaystyle f_{U}}f_{U} and {\displaystyle {f'}_{U'}}{\displaystyle {f'}_{U'}} coincide on the intersection {\displaystyle U\cap U'}U\cap U' (this is, in particular, vacuously true if the intersection is empty, but since {\displaystyle V}V is assumed irreducible, this is impossible). The proof that this defines an equivalence relation relies on the following lemma:

If two morphisms of varieties are equal on some non-empty open set, then they are equal.
{\displaystyle f}f is said to be birational if there exists a rational map {\displaystyle g\colon W\to V}g\colon W\to V which is its inverse, where the composition is taken in the above sense.

The importance of rational maps to algebraic geometry is in the connection between such maps and maps between the function fields of {\displaystyle V}V and {\displaystyle W}W. Even a cursory examination of the definitions reveals a similarity between that of rational map and that of rational function; in fact, a rational function is just a rational map whose range is the projective line. Composition of functions then allows us to "pull back" rational functions along a rational map, so that a single rational map {\displaystyle f\colon V\to W}f\colon V\to W induces a homomorphism of fields {\displaystyle K(W)\to K(V)}K(W)\to K(V). In particular, the following theorem is central: the functor from the category of projective varieties with dominant rational maps (over a fixed base field, for example {\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }\mathbb {C} ) to the category of finitely generated field extensions of the base field with reverse inclusion of extensions as morphisms, which associates each variety to its function field and each map to the associated map of function fields, is an equivalence of categories.1 / 1ChatGPTRational maps are an essential concept in algebraic geometry, which is the study of algebraic solutions to polynomial equations. It is just one of a number of such possible extensions.

Given a hypersurface {\displaystyle X\subset \mathbb {P} ^{n}}{\displaystyle X\subset \mathbb {P} ^{n}} defined by a degree {\displaystyle d}d polynomial {\displaystyle F}F and a rational {\displaystyle n}n-form {\displaystyle \omega }\omega  on {\displaystyle \mathbb {P} ^{n}}\mathbb {P} ^{n} with a pole of order {\displaystyle k>0}k>0 on {\displaystyle X}X, then we can construct a cohomology class {\displaystyle \operatorname {Res} (\omega )\in H^{n-1}(X;\mathbb {C} )}{\displaystyle \operatorname {Res} (\omega )\in H^{n-1}(X;\mathbb {C} )}. If {\displaystyle n=1}n=1 we recover the classical residue construction.1 / 1ChatGPTThe PoincarÃ© residue is a powerful tool in the realm of several complex variables and complex manifold theory, generalizing the notion of residue in the one-variable complex function theory.To give a basic overview:Complex Function Theory (Single Variable):
In the setting of functions of a single complex variable, the residue is a number associated with a singular point (pole) of a function, capturing the 'essence' of the behavior of the function near that pole. It's an integral concept in evaluating certain complex integrals.PoincarÃ© Residue (Several Complex Variables):
The PoincarÃ© residue extends this idea to the setting of several complex variables. The fundamental concept remains â we're looking at the behavior of a function near a special point or set â but the setting is richer and more complex.Definitions:The hypersurface XXX is a subset of projective space Pn\mathbb {P}^{n}Pn. If n=1n = 1n=1, the PoincarÃ© residue collapses to the classical residue, the one you'd encounter in a basic complex analysis course.In essence, while the residue in one-variable complex analysis provides a number at each pole of a function, the PoincarÃ© residue provides a richer structureâa cohomology classâfor functions (or forms) in the setting of several complex variables. This generalized concept keeps the spirit of capturing the local behavior of the function (or form) but does so in a manner appropriate to the more intricate setting of several variables.1 / 1In mathematics, the fixed-point index is a concept in topological fixed-point theory, and in particular Nielsen theory. The fixed-point index can be thought of as a multiplicity measurement for fixed points.

The index can be easily defined in the setting of complex analysis: Let f(z) be a holomorphic mapping on the complex plane, and let z0 be a fixed point of f. Then the function f(z) â z is holomorphic, and has an isolated zero at z0. We define the fixed-point index of f at z0, denoted i(f, z0), to be the multiplicity of the zero of the function f(z) â z at the point z0.

In real Euclidean space, the fixed-point index is defined as follows: If x0 is an isolated fixed point of f, then let g be the function defined by

{\displaystyle g(x)={\frac {x-f(x)}{||x-f(x)||}}.}{\displaystyle g(x)={\frac {x-f(x)}{||x-f(x)||}}.}
Then g has an isolated singularity at x0, and maps the boundary of some deleted neighborhood of x0 to the unit sphere. We define i(f, x0) to be the Brouwer degree of the mapping induced by g on some suitably chosen small sphere around x01 / 1ChatGPTThe fixed-point index is a foundational idea in topological fixed-point theory, which deals with finding points in a space that remain unchanged under a function (these are the fixed points). The fixed-point index helps us understand how many fixed points there are in a local neighborhood and the nature of these fixed points.In a nutshell:Intuition: Think of a point on a map. If, after applying a transformation (like stretching, bending, or rotating the map), the point remains where it originally was, then that point is a "fixed point". The fixed-point index gives us a deeper understanding of this point, especially in scenarios where the situation isn't straightforward.Complex Analysis: In the complex plane, we can associate a number, called the fixed-point index, to each fixed point of a holomorphic function. This index corresponds to the "multiplicity" of the fixed point. For instance, if f(z)=z3f(z) = z^3f(z)=z3, the function has a fixed point at z=0z = 0z=0 with an index of 3. This index essentially counts how many times the point is "fixed".Euclidean Space: In the realm of real numbers and vectors, the definition is more involved. Here, the fixed-point index measures how a function "twists" or "turns" around a fixed point. The function g(x)g(x)g(x) described in the provided definition is constructed to capture the local behavior of fff near the fixed point x0x_0x0â. The index is then the Brouwer degree of the mapping by ggg on a small sphere around x0x_0x0â, and it captures how many times ggg wraps the sphere around the fixed point.Applications: The fixed-point index plays a role in various results in mathematics. For example, it's used in proving the Brouwer fixed-point theorem, which states that any continuous function from a closed ball in RnR^nRn to itself has a fixed point. The index also helps in classifying the types of fixed points a system has, which can be crucial in fields like dynamical systems and differential equations.In essence, while the idea of a fixed point is intuitive and straightforward, the fixed-point index provides a more refined tool to understand the nuances of such points, especially in higher dimensions or in more complex settings.1 / 1A list of all of the topics we have discussed so far, just the headings.1 / 1ChatGPTOf course! If you have any further questions or need more information on the topic, feel free to ask!1 / 1An explanation of what they each are1 / 1ChatGPTCertainly!Symplectic Manifolds:
A symplectic manifold is a smooth manifold equipped with a closed, non-degenerate 2-form. This 2-form, called a symplectic form, allows one to make precise the notion of "phase space" in classical mechanics. Phase space is a space in which all possible states of a system are represented, with each state corresponding to a unique point in the phase space. Symplectic manifolds play a foundational role in Hamiltonian mechanics, where the evolution of a system can be described using Hamilton's equations. These equations are naturally derived from the symplectic structure of the phase space.Pseudoholomorphic Curves:
A pseudoholomorphic curve in a symplectic manifold is a curve (a map from a Riemann surface to the manifold) that satisfies the Cauchy-Riemann equations when the manifold is equipped with an almost complex structure compatible with the symplectic form. These curves are of fundamental importance in symplectic topology. They were introduced by Mikhail Gromov and have been a powerful tool in studying the properties of symplectic manifolds, leading to breakthroughs in areas such as the Gromov non-squeezing theorem and the definition of GromovâWitten invariants.In simpler terms:A symplectic manifold is like the stage where the dance of classical mechanics plays out.Pseudoholomorphic curves are special curves on this stage that have been instrumental in understanding the choreography of this dance in deeper ways.Both concepts are central in modern mathematical physics, especially in the study of classical and quantum mechanics.1 / 1Sign and geometric meaning of curvature

equally apply to learning in place of mathematics. A universal learning system,

degrees of freedom (where, apparently, only two are ef f ectively used.) Human

forwards.

(Icosahedron, one of the i ve Platonic bodies, is a convex polyhedron with 20 
triangular faces, 30 edges and 12 vertices, where each vertex has 5 faces adjacent 
to it. Its symmetry group consists of 120 elements: 60 rotations around the 
center and 60 = 30+30 mirror ref l ections with respect to the 30 planes normally 
bisecting the edges and 30 bisecting the dihedral angles at the edges. This is,

Here are the main points:Eyes and Optics: The spherical structure of vertebrate eyes, as well as the eyes of cephalopods like squids, resembles simple cameras. The universal structural similarity of eyes, whether in humans or squids, arises from the constraints imposed by the geometry and optics of our three-dimensional Euclidean space. Humans and squids use different methods to focus their eyes (changing lens shape vs. moving the lens), but the common structure reflects the shared geometric and optical principles.Wings and Aerodynamics: The architecture of wings in birds and bats exhibits universality due to the constraints of aerodynamics. While wings have evolved from limbs, the overall structural and functional similarity between bird and bat wings arises from the requirements of flight. Insects, which are the only invertebrates capable of flight, have different wing architectures and flying patterns due to their unique aerodynamic challenges. Hummingbirds, despite being distinct from insects, have their own unique modes of flight similar to but different from insects.Viral Symmetry and Icosahedra: The prediction of icosahedral symmetry in viruses was made by scientists Crick, Watson, Caspar, and Klug in 1956 based on mathematical reasoning and early analysis of X-ray diffraction patterns. An icosahedron is a polyhedron with 20 triangular faces, 30 edges, and 12 vertices, and it exhibits maximal rotational symmetry in three-dimensional space.Overall, the passage highlights how various biological structures, whether eyes, wings, or viral symmetry, display universal features rooted in physics, geometry, and the constraints of their respective environments.1 / 1Why does the random mutation/selection evolutionary mechanism generates

The protein subunits and nucleic acid chains spontaneously come together to form virus particles in their lowest energy state. This self-assembly leads to symmetrical structures that reflect the spatial symmetry of the physical laws governing them. For example, spherical shapes are often found in small liquid drops due to their minimal surface area surrounding a given volume. This connection between symmetry and energy minimization is also observed in crystals formed by non-symmetric protein molecules, where the paradox of non-symmetric molecules creating symmetric structures arises.Helical Symmetry: Molecular assemblies often exhibit helical symmetry. This occurs when two molecular subunits of the same species preferentially bind to each other in a specific manner. The binding energy for such a pair of molecules has a unique minimum when they adopt a helical arrangement. This phenomenon can be seen in biological structures like viral capsids and some protein filaments. The helical symmetry emerges due to the specific binding preferences and interactions between the molecules.In summary, the inherent properties of the molecules and their interactions, combined with the principles of energy minimization and the laws of statistical mechanics, contribute to the generation of improbable symmetries in biological structures.1 / 12: Information. Jun 13, 2018


Could studying swarm behaviors teach us how to help drones fly safely?

by Tom Abate, Glen Martin , Stanford University




A starling roost in the United Kingdom. Credit: Unsplash/James Wainscoat

Anyone who's seen a flock of starlings twist and turn across the sky may have wondered: How do they maneuver in such close formation without colliding?

"Many types of animals swarm or flock or otherwise move in coordinated ways," says Nicholas Ouellette, an associate professor of civil and environmental engineering at Stanford. "No individual animal knows what every other animal is doing, yet somehow they move cohesively as a group."

Understanding precisely how they do this, Ouellette says, may be a key to helping engineers design "flocks" of aerial drones and driverless cars. Emulating animal swarms is attractive because they not only operate without central control but they're also fault-tolerant, to use an engineering term, meaning they can adapt quickly and gracefully to sudden or unexpected conditions. Swarms are also resilient in that they can operate in dirty, disturbed environments.

So Ouellette and his team have embarked on a series of studies that explore how animal swarms develop the kind of self-organized and self-regulated systems that would allow devices such as drones and mobile sensors to operate safely and efficiently, without the sort of top-down controls that typify, for example, something like the air traffic control system.

Rather than examine starlings or other birds, which would be impractical to study in the lab because of the space they need to fly, Ouellette and his crew look at non-biting midges, a type of flying insect often found near water or in the shade of trees. Unlike starlings, whose graceful formations, called murmurations, make us stare up at the sky, midges buzz around in chaotic, cloud-like masses. But, says Ouellette, midge swarms still share common features with bird flocks and other animal groups, in that the swarms stay cohesive without any outside control or leadership.

"Midges are so small, they're easy to keep in a lab," Ouellette says. "They swarm at dusk and dawn and are easy to cue with lights." In his lab, the midges live in a roughly 5-foot plastic cube, surrounded by high-speed cameras that he uses to reconstruct 3-D trajectories for each insectâdirection, accelerationâall the essential kinematic information needed to characterize swarm movements.

Working in the lab allows Ouellette's team to run experiments to test different models. The team starts with observed data taken from the camera images. Then they use this data to test hypotheses about what sorts of rules might govern midge movements. But because natural swarm behavior does not provide enough information to distinguish between many hypotheses, the researchers also disrupt the swarm using light and sound to observe whether the real swarm is affected as their proposed rules had predicted.

Ouellette says it's too early to design artificial systems that behave the same way as animal groups. But his team is developing the sort of experimental environment that other researchers may one day use to build and test high-tech systems based on the bottom-up wisdom of the swarms rather than the top-down rules that typify many technological undertakings.

"As engineers we like to control things," Ouellette says. "But we have a lot to learn from insects and other animals that operate just fine without human command and control frameworks

These animal swarms exhibit cohesive movements without centralized control, which makes them attractive models for designing fault-tolerant and adaptable systems.Nicholas Ouellette and his team are conducting studies using non-biting midges, a type of flying insect, as a model for swarm behavior. Unlike birds, midges are small and can be easily studied in a lab environment. The researchers use high-speed cameras to track and analyze the 3D trajectories of individual midges within a swarm. By disrupting the swarm in controlled experiments, they can test hypotheses about the rules that govern swarm movements and observe how the real swarm reacts.The goal of this research is to develop an understanding of the principles underlying animal swarming behaviors, and eventually apply these insights to design artificial systems that can operate without centralized control. This approach contrasts with the traditional top-down control frameworks commonly used in technology, as it seeks to harness the collective wisdom of swarms to create more adaptive and resilient autonomous systems.1 / 1Ways that cellular automata and Ising synchs can model swarm behavior1 / 1ChatGPTCellular automata and the Ising model are two computational approaches that can be used to model swarm behavior in different ways:Cellular Automata (CA): Cellular automata are discrete models that consist of a grid of cells, each with a state that can evolve over discrete time steps based on a set of predefined rules. CA can simulate complex behaviors emerging from simple rules. To model swarm behavior using cellular automata:Individual Rules: Each cell represents an individual agent, and its state represents its behavior or movement. The rules for state transitions can be designed to mimic the local interactions and behaviors observed in real swarms.Neighborhood Interactions: The state transition rules can take into account the states of neighboring cells, representing the interactions among swarm members. For example, each cell could adjust its state based on the states of its neighboring cells.Emergent Patterns: Through interactions and rule-based updates, emergent swarm behaviors can arise, such as flocking, clustering, and pattern formation. These behaviors can be studied to understand how local interactions give rise to global swarm behavior.Ising Model: The Ising model is a mathematical model used in statistical mechanics to study the behavior of magnetic spins in a lattice. It can also be adapted to model collective behavior, including swarm behavior, by considering the alignment of agents' states.Spin Alignment: In the Ising model, each agent or "spin" has a binary state (up or down). Similarly, each swarm member can be assigned a state that represents its behavior or direction (e.g., moving left or right).Interaction Energies: Interactions between neighboring agents are characterized by interaction energies that influence their states. Positive energies encourage alignment, while negative energies favor misalignment.Temperature Parameter: The temperature parameter controls the balance between alignment and randomness. At higher temperatures, agents are more likely to change their states, resembling more chaotic or exploratory behavior. At lower temperatures, alignment dominates.Phase Transitions: By varying the temperature parameter, the model can exhibit phase transitions, where swarm behavior transitions from disordered to ordered states, resembling the emergence of coordinated swarming behaviors.Both cellular automata and the Ising model provide a framework to explore the emergence of swarm behavior through simple rules and interactions. They allow researchers to study how local interactions among agents lead to the emergence of complex and coordinated global behaviors observed in natural swarms.1 / 1Connections with the prievous topics.1 / 1ChatGPTThe discussion you provided earlier about viral symmetry, crystal lattices, and mathematical constraints in biological systems can be related to the modeling of swarm behavior using cellular automata and the Ising model in the context of emergent behaviors:Symmetry and Emergent Behavior: Just as the symmetry of viruses is influenced by the underlying physical laws and energy considerations, the emergent behavior of swarms can also be influenced by the local interactions and constraints present in the environment.
